The cost of the information vacuum is already being paid: $650M/year in higher municipal borrowing costs (Brookings), billions in undetected government waste (University of Chicago), and incalculable democratic costs from uninformed electorates.

**8b. Platform accountability.**

Require algorithmic transparency from platforms above 50 million U.S. users: mandatory disclosure of recommendation algorithms, independent auditing of algorithmic amplification effects, prohibition on algorithmic targeting of political content to minors, and mandatory "chronological feed" option for all users. Require platforms to provide researcher access to data on content distribution and amplification patterns, with appropriate privacy protections. Establish a Digital Platform Accountability Office within the FTC with dedicated staffing and expertise.

*Evidence*: Internal Facebook documents (the 2021 Frances Haugen disclosures) confirmed that the company's own research showed its algorithms amplified divisive and outrage-inducing content, that it was aware of the harm, and that it chose engagement metrics over user welfare. Algorithmic amplification is not neutral curation — it is an editorial decision made at scale, and it should be subject to accountability proportional to its influence. A 2024 NYU study found that platform recommender systems increase user exposure to misinformation by 70% compared to chronological feeds.

*Precedent*: The EU's Digital Services Act (DSA, 2022) provides a working model: it requires very large platforms (VLOPs, those with 45M+ EU users) to conduct systemic risk assessments, submit to independent audits, and provide researcher access to data. The European Commission designated 19 VLOPs in 2023 and has already opened formal proceedings against X/Twitter and TikTok. Australia's Online Safety Act (2021) created an eSafety Commissioner with investigative and enforcement powers. South Korea's Telecommunications Business Act requires platforms to disclose recommendation algorithm criteria and provide opt-out mechanisms.

*Implementation pathway*: Federal statute. The U.S. can adopt a DSA-equivalent framework adapted to First Amendment requirements — focusing on transparency and accountability for algorithmic systems rather than content regulation.

*Fiscal impact*: Digital Platform Accountability Office: $200-400M/year (comparable to the EU's DSA enforcement infrastructure). Revenue from platform accountability fines (the EU has assessed potential penalties in the billions under the DSA) would offset. The societal cost of unregulated algorithmic amplification — measured in political polarization, mental health impacts ($11B/year estimated cost for adolescent mental health effects alone, per the Surgeon General's 2023 advisory), and democratic erosion — dwarfs the regulatory investment.

**8c. Civic information infrastructure.**

Fund the development of community information networks — local group chats, email lists, community radio, digital town squares — that provide reliable local information when national media is compromised. This is information infrastructure, not content regulation. The goal is resilient local information flows that survive the failure of any single platform or media outlet. Establish a Community Information Fund ($500M/year) for grants to local civic technology projects, community radio stations, and neighborhood information networks.

*Evidence*: Taiwan's g0v civic tech community and the vTaiwan deliberation platform demonstrate that civic information infrastructure can be built as public goods. During the COVID-19 pandemic, Taiwan's civic tech community built mask availability maps, real-time information dashboards, and fact-checking infrastructure faster than the government could have — because the infrastructure was designed to enable citizen participation rather than centralized broadcast. Barcelona's Decidim platform (400+ organizations, 150,000+ participants) demonstrates that open-source civic technology can operate at metropolitan scale. In the U.S., community radio (1,500+ LPFM stations) and the emerging network of local nonprofit newsrooms provide a foundation to build on. The Knight Foundation's research shows that communities with strong local information ecosystems have 25-40% higher civic engagement rates.

*Precedent*: South Korea's Community Media Foundation provides government funding for community broadcasting and citizen journalism training, operating 18 community media centers across the country. The UK's Community Radio Fund (Ofcom-administered) has supported 300+ community stations since 2004. Canada's Community Access Program provided public internet access and digital literacy training to underserved communities before its 2012 defunding — a cautionary example of what happens when civic infrastructure lacks protected funding.

*Fiscal impact*: Community Information Fund: $500M/year. Community radio infrastructure: $100M/year. Digital civic infrastructure (open-source platforms, hosting, maintenance): $50-100M/year. Total: $650-700M/year — approximately $2 per citizen annually. The return is measured in civic engagement, government accountability, and community resilience rather than direct fiscal savings.

**8d. Press freedom protections.**

Establish by statute a federal shield law protecting journalists from compelled disclosure of sources, with narrow exceptions only for imminent physical harm. Criminalize the use of federal resources to surveil, intimidate, or retaliate against journalists. Create an independent press freedom ombudsman with authority to investigate complaints, publish findings, and refer cases for prosecution. Mandate that all federal law enforcement agencies adopt specific protocols for interactions with journalists at protests and public events, including a prohibition on targeting press equipment and a requirement for designated press liaisons at major events.

*Evidence*: The RSF reclassification and the 170 documented assaults on journalists in 2025 (predominantly by law enforcement) indicate that informal norms protecting press freedom have collapsed. Statutory protection is required because the First Amendment, while providing a constitutional floor, has not prevented the pattern of intimidation and violence documented in the crisis analysis. Only 34 states and the District of Columbia have shield laws; the absence of a federal shield law means that federal prosecutors can compel journalists to reveal sources in federal investigations — a power that has been used to chill national security reporting.

*Precedent*: Sweden's Freedom of the Press Act (Tryckfrihetsförordningen, 1766) is the world's oldest press freedom law and is part of Sweden's constitution — making press freedom a fundamental law that cannot be altered by ordinary legislation. It includes absolute source protection: it is a criminal offense for any government official to attempt to identify a journalist's source, and journalists face imprisonment if they voluntarily disclose sources. Norway's constitution (Section 100) provides explicit press freedom protections, and the Norwegian Media Authority operates independently of the government. The European Court of Human Rights has consistently held that source protection is a fundamental condition for press freedom (Goodwin v. United Kingdom, 1996; Voskuil v. Netherlands, 2007).

*Fiscal impact*: Press freedom ombudsman office: $20-50M/year. Law enforcement press interaction training: $30-50M/year through DOJ grants. The fiscal return of press freedom — measured in corruption deterred, government accountability maintained, and informed democratic participation enabled — is incalculable. The empirical evidence cited in 8a (higher borrowing costs, increased waste, lower turnout) demonstrates the measurable costs when press freedom erodes.

**8e. Media literacy and critical thinking education.**

Mandate and fund media literacy education in all federally funded schools, starting in elementary school and continuing through secondary education. Curriculum should cover: identifying reliable sources, understanding algorithmic curation, recognizing disinformation techniques, distinguishing news from opinion, understanding media ownership and business models, and developing habits of verification before sharing. Fund teacher training, curriculum development, and ongoing assessment of media literacy outcomes. Connect to the civic education reforms in Domain 10.

*Evidence*: Stanford University's evaluations of student media literacy (2016, 2019, 2022) found that a majority of U.S. students at every level — from middle school through college — could not reliably distinguish news articles from advertisements, identify the source of a claim, or evaluate the credibility of a website. The National Association for Media Literacy Education reports that only 14 states include media literacy in their educational standards, and most of those do so superficially. Meanwhile, the proliferation of AI-generated content, deepfakes, and synthetic media is accelerating — making media literacy not a supplementary skill but a survival capability for democratic citizenship.

*Precedent*: Finland's media literacy education program is the global gold standard. Media literacy has been integrated into the national curriculum since the 1970s and was made a mandatory cross-curricular theme in 2016. Finnish students learn to analyze media from first grade through high school — covering source evaluation, understanding bias, recognizing propaganda techniques, and creating their own media content. Finland consistently ranks #1 on the Open Society Institute's Media Literacy Index and #1-3 on the World Press Freedom Index. The correlation is not coincidental: citizens who can evaluate information critically are both less susceptible to manipulation and more capable of holding media accountable. Estonia's media literacy program, developed after Russian disinformation campaigns targeting the Russian-speaking minority, has become a model for digital resilience — integrating cybersecurity, source verification, and critical thinking into a unified curriculum. Canada's MediaSmarts (formerly the Media Awareness Network), a government-funded nonprofit, has developed K-12 media literacy resources used in all provinces and territories.

*Implementation pathway*: Federal statute conditioning education funding (ESEA/ESSA) on adoption of evidence-based media literacy standards, with federal grants for curriculum development, teacher training, and assessment tools. The Department of Education (or its successor, if abolished and reformed per Domain 10) would develop model curricula in partnership with media literacy organizations and academic researchers.

*Fiscal impact*: Federal grants for curriculum development and teacher training: $200-400M/year. Ongoing assessment and program evaluation: $50M/year. Total: $250-450M/year — less than $1.50 per citizen annually. The return is measured in democratic resilience: Finland's investment in media literacy has made it the most resistant country in Europe to disinformation campaigns, despite sharing a 1,340-kilometer border with Russia and being a primary target of Russian information operations.

---

## Domain 9: Federalism and Local Democracy

### The Problem

American federalism is simultaneously a strength (distributed power, policy experimentation, local responsiveness) and a vulnerability (states can suppress voting rights, restrict civil liberties, and entrench minority rule). The current crisis has exposed both dimensions with unusual clarity — and revealed a third problem: the systematic destruction of local democratic authority by state governments that claim to champion local control.

**Federalism as rights suppression.** Since Shelby County v. Holder (2013) weakened federal voting rights oversight, states have used their authority over elections to restrict voting access with surgical precision. The Brennan Center documented 94 restrictive voting laws enacted in 29 states between 2013 and 2024. Texas closed 750 polling places, disproportionately in communities of color. Georgia's SB 202 (2021) restricted ballot drop boxes, limited weekend voting, and criminalized distributing food and water to voters in line. Florida's SB 90 restricted vote-by-mail access and imposed new ID requirements for ballot requests. The pattern is consistent: states use federalism's flexibility to restrict the franchise of populations that threaten incumbent power. Without a strong federal rights floor, federalism becomes the mechanism through which democratic backsliding occurs — not despite local control, but through it.

**State preemption of local democracy.** A growing pattern of state legislatures preempting municipal authority has created what the National League of Cities calls "the most significant threat to local self-governance in a generation." The pattern is systematic: cities enact progressive policies (minimum wage increases, paid leave, tenant protections, plastic bag bans, gun safety measures, LGBTQ+ nondiscrimination ordinances), and state legislatures, often gerrymandered to over-represent rural constituencies, pass preemption laws that void those local decisions. The Grassroots Change Preemption Watch project documents over 500 instances of state preemption of local authority since 2000. Twenty-five states have preempted local minimum wage laws; 43 have preempted some form of local gun regulation; 21 have preempted local nondiscrimination ordinances; 18 have preempted local rent control. The irony is structural: the same state legislators who invoke federalism and "local control" when resisting federal standards actively destroy local control within their own states. Research by the Economic Policy Institute shows that state preemption of local minimum wages has cost workers in preempted cities an estimated $1.5 billion annually.

**Fragmented governance as service failure.** The United States has approximately 90,000 units of local government — more than any other developed country by a large margin. This fragmentation produces duplicated services, uneven quality, fiscal competition between jurisdictions (the "race to the bottom" on taxes and regulation), and governance structures so complex that democratic accountability becomes effectively impossible. The average American is governed by 10-14 overlapping jurisdictions (city/town, county, school district, water district, fire district, transit authority, etc.), most of which hold separate elections with turnout rates below 15%. A citizen who wanted to attend every public meeting of every body that governs them would need to attend meetings multiple times per week — a practical impossibility. The result is governance without meaningful democratic participation: decisions about schools, water, policing, and infrastructure are made by bodies that most citizens cannot name, in meetings most citizens do not know occur.

**The interstate coordination gap.** Many of the most pressing policy challenges — climate change, water management, transportation, public health, labor standards — cross state lines and cannot be effectively addressed by individual states. The current federal system offers limited mechanisms for interstate coordination: interstate compacts require congressional consent (often delayed for years), regional planning agencies lack enforcement authority, and voluntary cooperation agreements have no binding force. The result is a governance gap where problems that are too large for states but too politically contentious for Congress fall through the cracks — addressed inadequately or not at all.

**The democratic consequence.** Federalism is not intrinsically democratic — it is a structural choice that can either enable or suppress democratic governance depending on its design. American federalism currently operates with too few rights floors (allowing states to suppress voting, civil liberties, and local authority), too much fragmentation (preventing effective governance and meaningful civic participation), and too little coordination capacity (leaving regional and national challenges unaddressed). Reforming federalism is not about centralizing power — it is about ensuring that every level of government operates democratically and that citizens have meaningful input at every level that affects their lives.

### Proposed Reforms

**9a. Subsidiarity principle with a non-derogable rights floor.**

Codify the principle that decisions should be made at the lowest effective level of government, with a constitutional rights floor that no state or local government can fall below. States retain autonomy on education, healthcare delivery, infrastructure, and economic development. They cannot opt out of equal protection, due process, voting rights, civil liberties, or minimum labor and environmental standards. The rights floor should be enforceable through private right of action in federal court — citizens should not depend on DOJ enforcement alone, which is vulnerable to political capture.

*Precedent*: Switzerland — 26 cantons, 2,131 communes, direct democracy at multiple levels. Citizens vote four times per year on average. Cantons have substantial autonomy on education, healthcare, policing, and taxation. But the federal constitution establishes non-derogable rights (Articles 7-36) that no canton can fall below, enforced by the Federal Supreme Court. The system has operated since 1848 with the highest civic trust in Europe and consistently ranks #1-3 globally for democratic quality (V-Dem, EIU Democracy Index). Germany's Basic Law (Article 28) requires that all state (Länder) constitutions conform to "principles of a republican, democratic, and social state governed by the rule of law" — creating a federal guarantee of democratic governance at every level. The EU's Charter of Fundamental Rights establishes a rights floor that member states cannot derogate from, enforced by the Court of Justice of the European Union — the most ambitious multi-level rights floor in operation. Canada's Charter of Rights and Freedoms (1982) overrides provincial legislation that violates fundamental rights, with the "notwithstanding clause" (Section 33) creating a narrow, politically costly mechanism for override. (See `from-scratch-design.md`, Model G4.)

*Evidence*: The contrast between states with strong rights floors and those without is measurable. States that maintained voting rights protections after Shelby County have higher turnout, more competitive elections, and greater policy responsiveness to median voter preferences (Hajnal, Lajevardi, and Nielson, 2017). States that have enacted restrictive voting laws since 2013 show measurable declines in turnout among affected populations — up to 7% in communities most affected by polling place closures (Texas LULAC analysis). The pattern holds across policy domains: states without minimum labor standards have lower wages, worse working conditions, and higher poverty rates even after controlling for economic structure.

*Implementation pathway*: Federal statute establishing minimum standards in voting access, labor, environment, and civil liberties — modeled on the Voting Rights Act's enforcement mechanism but extended to other domains. Constitutional amendment for permanent protection. Estimated timeline: statute within 2-4 years; amendment over 10-20 years.

*Fiscal impact*: Negligible direct federal cost — the reform primarily constrains state action. Federal enforcement infrastructure (expanded DOJ Civil Rights Division, plus the racial equity office proposed in Domain 22d): $300-500M/year, funded through existing enforcement budgets. The fiscal return is in avoided costs: states that suppress voting rights and civil liberties impose costs on the federal system through increased litigation ($500M+ annually in voting rights litigation alone), reduced economic participation, and diminished tax revenue from excluded populations.

**9b. State-level laboratories of democracy.**

Encourage states to serve as testing grounds for structural reforms: proportional representation, citizens' assemblies, digital government, participatory budgeting, LVT pilots, ranked-choice voting. Establish a Federal Democratic Innovation Fund that provides matching grants (2:1 federal-to-state) for states that adopt evidence-based democratic innovations, with independent evaluation and public reporting of results. Success at state level creates demonstration effects that build federal momentum.

*Evidence*: Maine adopted ranked-choice voting (2018). Alaska adopted open primaries with RCV (2022). Oregon pioneered automatic voter registration (2015 — now adopted by 23 states). California and Michigan implemented independent redistricting commissions (2008, 2018). New York City implemented participatory budgeting (2011). Every major democratic reform in U.S. history started at the state or local level before going national — women's suffrage (Wyoming 1869 → 19th Amendment 1920), direct election of senators (Oregon 1904 → 17th Amendment 1913), marijuana legalization (Colorado/Washington 2012 → 24 states by 2024), marriage equality (Massachusetts 2004 → Obergefell 2015). The pattern is consistent: local success creates demonstration effects, builds public familiarity, and generates the political constituency for national adoption.

*Precedent*: Germany's federalism explicitly encourages policy experimentation (Experimentierklausel — "experimentation clauses" in federal law that allow states to deviate from federal standards to test alternative approaches, with mandatory evaluation). Finland's experimental legislation framework allows time-limited policy experiments with built-in evaluation — the universal basic income trial (2017-2018, 2,000 participants) was conducted under this authority. Canada's asymmetric federalism allows different provinces to assume different federal functions (Quebec administers its own pension plan, immigration selection, and income tax collection), creating natural experiments in governance approaches.

*Implementation pathway*: Federal statute establishing the Democratic Innovation Fund. Modeled on the Race to the Top education fund (2009-2015), which successfully incentivized state-level education reform through competitive grants — but with democratic innovation rather than education as the focus. Estimated annual cost: $500M-1B in matching grants.

*Fiscal impact*: Democratic Innovation Fund: $500M-1B/year. The return is structural rather than fiscal: each successful state-level innovation builds the evidence base and political constituency for national adoption. Oregon's automatic voter registration, for example, has added over 500,000 new registrants and is estimated to have increased turnout by 4-6% — with effectively zero ongoing cost after implementation. The cumulative fiscal impact of democratic innovations (reduced corruption from participatory budgeting, more responsive fiscal policy from better representation, efficiency gains from digital government) is estimated at multiples of the investment, though precise estimates depend on adoption rates.

**9c. Municipal reform pathways.**

Many of the most impactful reforms — participatory budgeting, citizens' assemblies, open government data, digital services, ranked-choice voting — can be implemented at the municipal level without state or federal action. A coordinated movement of municipal reform creates facts on the ground that change what voters consider possible. Establish a National Municipal Innovation Network (modeled on the Bloomberg Philanthropies' What Works Cities program) to provide technical assistance, evaluation frameworks, and peer learning for cities implementing democratic innovations.

*Evidence*: The progressive era's most durable reforms — direct election of senators, women's suffrage, the initiative and referendum — all began as state and local experiments before going national. More recently: marijuana legalization started in two states (2012) and has spread to 24. Marriage equality was legal in 36 states before the Supreme Court nationalized it. Ranked-choice voting was used in one state (Maine, 2018) and has expanded to Alaska, New York City, and over 50 jurisdictions. The pattern is consistent — local success creates demonstration effects, builds public familiarity, and generates the political constituency for national adoption.

*Precedent*: Barcelona's "municipalism" movement (2015-present) demonstrated that a city government can implement radical democratic innovations — the Decidim digital deliberation platform (400+ organizations, 150,000+ participants), neighborhood assemblies, participatory budgeting, and civic technology infrastructure — that become models for national reform. South Korea's local government innovations, including the Suwon Citizen Governance initiative and the Seoul Innovation Park, have informed national policy. In the U.S., the Bloomberg What Works Cities program (2015-present) certified 97 cities for evidence-based governance practices, creating a peer network that accelerates adoption of proven approaches.

*Implementation pathway*: Federal support through the Municipal Innovation Network ($200M/year) plus technical assistance. Protection from state preemption (see 9d) is the essential precondition — without it, innovative cities face legal risk from hostile state legislatures.

*Fiscal impact*: National Municipal Innovation Network: $200M/year. The return: cities that adopt evidence-based governance practices consistently show improved service delivery, reduced costs, and higher civic engagement. New York City's participatory budgeting program allocated $300M+ through direct citizen votes, with independent evaluation showing high participation in low-income communities and spending decisions that better reflect community priorities than traditional budgeting. The overall fiscal impact of municipal innovation is multiplicative — each successful model reduces the political and technical cost of adoption for the next city.

**9d. Federal preemption reform.**

Establish clear principles for when the federal government may preempt state law, and — equally important — when it may not. Currently, federal preemption operates inconsistently: Congress preempts state consumer protection laws that exceed federal standards (benefiting industry) while allowing states to fall below federal labor, environmental, and civil rights standards (also benefiting industry). Reform should follow a one-way ratchet principle: states may exceed federal protections but may not fall below them. Federal legislation should include express preemption clauses rather than relying on implied preemption, and courts should apply a presumption against preemption in cases involving traditional state police powers.

Prohibit state preemption of local authority on core governance issues. A growing pattern of state legislatures preempting city-level minimum wages, paid leave, plastic bag bans, tenant protections, and gun regulations has created a "preemption penalty" that prevents the municipal experimentation that 9b and 9c rely on. Twenty-five states have preempted local minimum wage laws; 43 have preempted some form of local gun regulation; 21 have preempted local nondiscrimination ordinances. This is the federalism problem in reverse — and it must be addressed at both the state and federal level.

*Evidence*: The Grassroots Change Preemption Watch project documents over 500 instances of state preemption of local authority since 2000 — with the pace accelerating: more preemption laws were enacted between 2016 and 2024 than in the entire preceding decade. Research by the Economic Policy Institute shows that state preemption of local minimum wages has cost workers in preempted cities an estimated $1.5 billion annually. The National League of Cities has identified preemption as the single largest threat to local democratic governance. A 2023 study by the Local Solutions Support Center documented that state preemption laws disproportionately target policies enacted by cities with large minority populations — replicating the historical pattern of state legislatures overriding local governance in communities of color (Birmingham, Alabama's 2015 minimum wage increase was immediately preempted by the state legislature, which passed a law banning local minimum wage ordinances; the city is 70% Black).

*Precedent*: The EU's subsidiarity principle (Article 5, Treaty on European Union) provides the most developed framework for multi-level governance: the EU may act only when objectives "cannot be sufficiently achieved by the Member States" and can be "better achieved at Union level." National parliaments have a formal mechanism (the "yellow card" and "orange card" procedures) to challenge EU actions that violate subsidiarity. Germany's Basic Law (Article 30) establishes a presumption that state authority is primary unless specifically assigned to the federal level, with the Federal Constitutional Court actively enforcing this principle — but simultaneously prohibiting states from derogating from fundamental rights protections. Brazil's 1988 constitution explicitly protects municipal autonomy (Articles 29-30), including the right of municipalities to enact legislation on matters of local interest — making state preemption of municipal authority unconstitutional.

*Implementation pathway*: Federal statute establishing the one-way ratchet principle and protecting core municipal authority. State constitutional amendments or ballot initiatives to protect local home rule in states where preemption is most aggressive. Model legislation has been drafted by the National League of Cities and the Local Solutions Support Center.

*Fiscal impact*: Negligible direct federal cost — the reform is primarily structural. The economic impact of reversing preemption is substantial: restoring local minimum wage authority alone would benefit an estimated 3-5 million workers and increase annual wages by $1.5-3B (EPI estimate). Local paid leave policies, where preempted, would cover an estimated 4 million additional workers. The democratic cost of preemption — measured in reduced civic engagement, lower trust in government, and suppressed policy innovation — is difficult to quantify but significant.

**9e. Interstate compact frameworks for regional challenges.**

Facilitate interstate compacts — constitutionally authorized agreements between states — as a mechanism for addressing challenges that cross state lines but do not require federal legislation. Priority areas: regional water management (already partially addressed by existing compacts but inadequate for climate change), multistate carbon markets (the Regional Greenhouse Gas Initiative model), cross-border transit authorities, interstate professional licensing reciprocity, and coordinated emergency management.

Create a federal Interstate Compact Commission to provide technical assistance, mediation, and model compact language. Streamline the congressional consent process (currently ad hoc and often delayed for years) with expedited review for compacts that do not expand state power at federal expense.

*Evidence*: The National Popular Vote Interstate Compact — an agreement among states to award electoral votes to the national popular vote winner — demonstrates how compacts can achieve structural reform without constitutional amendment. Currently at 209 of the 270 electoral votes needed. The Driver License Compact (all 50 states) and the Interstate Compact for Juveniles (all 50 states) show that compacts can achieve universal adoption when the coordination benefits are clear. The Regional Greenhouse Gas Initiative (RGGI), a compact among 12 northeastern states, has reduced power sector CO2 emissions by over 50% since 2005 while generating $7.4 billion in allowance auction proceeds invested in clean energy, energy efficiency, and consumer benefits — demonstrating that interstate compacts can achieve policy outcomes comparable to federal legislation.

*Precedent*: Australia's Council of Australian Governments (COAG, now the National Cabinet) provides a formal framework for inter-state coordination, with binding agreements on healthcare, education, and economic reform. Canada's Council of the Federation facilitates inter-provincial coordination, with binding agreements on healthcare transfers, labor mobility, and trade. The EU's "enhanced cooperation" mechanism allows a subset of member states to pursue deeper integration in specific policy areas — a model for allowing willing U.S. states to cooperate more deeply without requiring national consensus. Switzerland's inter-cantonal concordats cover higher education, police, and healthcare, with over 800 active inter-cantonal agreements creating what scholars call "cooperative federalism" — states solving shared problems without federal intervention.

*Implementation pathway*: Federal statute establishing the Interstate Compact Commission and streamlining congressional consent. Executive order encouraging federal agencies to facilitate interstate cooperation on shared challenges. Estimated timeline: Commission operational within 2 years; first new major compacts within 3-5 years.

*Fiscal impact*: Interstate Compact Commission: $50-100M/year. The return is in coordination gains that neither federal nor individual state action can achieve. RGGI's $7.4 billion in clean energy investment demonstrates the fiscal scale that compacts can achieve. Professional licensing reciprocity (estimated to affect 30% of U.S. workers who require some form of occupational license) could reduce labor market friction by $6-12B/year in reduced relocation costs, increased labor mobility, and reduced occupational licensing barriers. Regional water management compacts, updated for climate change projections, could prevent billions in conflict and infrastructure duplication.

## Domain 10: Education

### The Problem

The American public education system is failing its democratic function. Public schools were designed to produce informed, capable citizens — people who can evaluate evidence, participate in civic life, hold institutions accountable, and collaborate across difference. Instead, the system produces vast inequality, chronic underfunding in the communities that need it most, declining civic knowledge, and a workforce inadequately prepared for the economy that actually exists.

**Funding inequality.** The U.S. funds public education primarily through local property taxes, creating a direct link between community wealth and school quality. The richest 10% of school districts spend 2-3 times as much per pupil as the poorest 10%. This is not a feature of all systems — most OECD countries fund education nationally or regionally with explicit equalization formulas. The U.S. system systematically underfunds the schools that serve the children with the greatest needs.

**Civic education collapse.** Only 9 states require a full year of civics education. Average civics proficiency among 8th graders has declined steadily since 2014, with the 2022 NAEP assessment showing the largest single-assessment decline in the test's history. A 2023 Annenberg survey found that only 47% of American adults could name all three branches of government. Citizens who cannot identify basic governmental structures cannot meaningfully hold those structures accountable.

**Politicized curriculum.** Education has become a primary theater of the culture war. Book bans reached record levels in 2023-2024 (4,240 titles challenged, per ALA). Legislation restricting classroom discussion of race, gender, and history passed in 18+ states between 2021 and 2025. The result is not neutrality but intellectual impoverishment — students denied access to the factual and analytical tools required for democratic citizenship.

**Teacher crisis.** The U.S. faces a structural teacher shortage driven by low pay relative to comparable professions (teachers earn approximately 73.5% of what similarly educated professionals earn, per the Economic Policy Institute), deteriorating working conditions, politicized attacks on professional autonomy, and student debt burdens from required graduate education. The shortage is most acute in low-income communities, special education, and STEM — precisely where need is greatest.

**Higher education debt trap.** Student loan debt exceeds $1.77 trillion, carried by 44 million borrowers. The average balance at graduation exceeds $37,000. This debt constrains career choices, delays family formation and homeownership, and functions as a regressive wealth transfer from borrowers (disproportionately from lower-income families) to institutions. Meanwhile, state funding per student at public colleges has declined approximately 30% in inflation-adjusted terms since 2000, shifting costs directly to students.

**The democratic consequence.** An underfunded, unequal, civically impoverished education system does not produce the informed citizenry democracy requires. The structural reforms in Domains 1-9 depend on a population capable of engaging with them — voting in proportional elections, participating in citizens' assemblies, understanding tax policy, evaluating information. Education is not a separate domain. It is the foundation the entire renewal framework rests on.

### Proposed Reforms

**10a. Weighted per-pupil funding with federal equalization.**

Replace the property-tax-based funding model with a national weighted per-pupil formula that guarantees a funded floor for every student, with additional weights for poverty, English language learners, special education, and rural isolation. Federal equalization transfers supplement state funding so that the poorest districts receive at minimum the national median per-pupil expenditure.

*Precedent*: The Netherlands has funded education through central weighted per-pupil allocations since 1917, with no property tax component. Canada's provinces (particularly Alberta and Ontario) use equalization formulas that significantly narrow inter-district spending gaps. Within the U.S., New Jersey's Abbott v. Burke decisions required the state to fund the 31 poorest districts at the same per-pupil level as the wealthiest, producing measurable improvements in achievement and graduation rates in Abbott districts over 20 years.

*Implementation*: Federal statute establishing the equalization formula and funding mechanism. Phase in over 5 years. Fund through reallocated federal education spending (currently fragmented across 100+ categorical programs) plus new revenue from the fiscal reforms in Domain 5.

*Fiscal impact*: Closing the per-pupil spending gap for the bottom quartile of districts to the national median requires approximately $30–50B in new annual federal investment — the primary cost driver in the total Domain 10 investment (Section 5.5). New Jersey's Abbott equalization, which mandated comparable per-pupil funding for the 31 poorest districts, produced measurable gains in 4th grade reading and graduation rates over 20 years at a cost of roughly $2-3B in additional state appropriation per year. Georgetown's Center on Education and the Workforce estimates each one-percentage-point increase in high school graduation rates adds $90B in lifetime earnings for affected cohorts — the equity argument and the fiscal argument are identical.

**10b. Universal civics education: K-12 civic learning requirement.**

Mandate at least one semester of civics in middle school and one year in high school for all public schools receiving federal funding. Curriculum must include: structure of government at all levels, how laws and budgets are made, the Bill of Rights and its application, media literacy and source evaluation, history of democratic movements and civil rights, and structured deliberation (based on Deliberative Polling methodology). Assessment should emphasize evidence evaluation and argumentation, not rote memorization of dates.

*Precedent*: Finland's national curriculum requires civic education throughout K-12, with emphasis on practical participation (students run mock elections, analyze media, deliberate on real policy questions). Finland consistently scores at the top of international civic knowledge assessments (IEA ICCS). Illinois mandated a semester of civics including a service-learning component in 2015; early evaluations show improved civic knowledge and increased voter registration among affected cohorts.

*Evidence*: The CivXNow coalition's meta-analysis of civics education research found consistent positive effects of action-oriented civics on political knowledge, sense of political efficacy, and future civic participation. The effect is strongest for students from low-income families — civic education is a leveler.

*Fiscal impact*: Civics curriculum reform has negligible capital cost — it requires curriculum development, assessment infrastructure, and teacher professional development, not new facilities or staff. Federal implementation cost: $200–500M/year. The civic returns are not easily quantified in dollars, but CIRCLE (Center for Information and Research on Civic Learning and Engagement) finds that youth voters who received sustained civic education participate at 15-20 percentage points higher rates than those who did not — and the demographic most concentrated in low-performing schools (low-income, communities of color) has the most to gain from this equalizer.

**10c. Teacher compensation and professional autonomy.**

Establish a federal teacher minimum salary of $60,000 (indexed to inflation and adjusted for regional cost of living), funded through the equalization mechanism in 10a. Forgive remaining student debt for teachers who complete 5 years in high-need schools. Eliminate state legislation that micromanages curriculum content (book bans, topic restrictions) by tying federal education funding to professional autonomy standards: teachers, not legislators, determine pedagogical methods and materials within board-approved curriculum frameworks.

*Precedent*: Singapore recruits teachers from the top third of graduates, pays them comparably to engineers, provides 100 hours of annual professional development, and allows substantial pedagogical autonomy within a national framework. The result: consistently top-3 global performance in PISA assessments. Estonia, with far fewer resources, achieves similar results through high teacher status and professional trust. Within the U.S., the Washington D.C. IMPACT system demonstrated that performance-based compensation paired with professional support improves teacher retention and student outcomes.

*Fiscal impact*: Supplementing teacher salaries to a federal minimum of $60,000 (most states would need $5,000–$20,000/teacher added) and funding NHSC-style debt forgiveness for high-need school placements would require $15–25B annually in federal matching investment. The alternative is not cheap — districts currently spend $20,000–30,000 per vacancy on substitutes, emergency hiring, and recruitment in shortage areas, and the quality cost of below-median-performing teachers is estimated by Hanushek and Rivkin at $250,000 in reduced lifetime earnings per classroom of students over a career. Hanushek and Woessmann's cross-country analysis finds that moving to selective teacher recruitment (Finland's model) narrows student achievement gaps by approximately 20 percentage points across two decades — a gain valued at trillions in human capital.

**10d. Debt-free public higher education.**

Make tuition at public two-year and four-year colleges free for families earning under $125,000 (indexed to inflation), covering approximately 80% of American families. Fund through federal-state cost sharing (federal government covers 75%, states cover 25%, conditional on states maintaining current per-student funding levels to prevent cost-shifting). Expand Pell Grants to cover living expenses for low-income students, since tuition is only one barrier to access.

*Precedent*: Germany eliminated university tuition in 2014 across all 16 states. Enrollment increased, particularly among students from lower-income families, with no decline in educational quality. The cost is approximately 1.2% of GDP — a fraction of the economic benefit of a more educated workforce. Within the U.S., Tennessee Promise (free community college since 2014) increased enrollment by 25% and completion by 15% in its first five years.

*Evidence*: Federal Reserve research consistently finds that student debt suppresses homeownership, business formation, and consumption. The drag on GDP from the current debt burden is estimated at 0.1-0.2% annually — roughly $25-50 billion in lost economic activity.

*Fiscal impact*: The Department of Education estimates free public tuition for families under $125,000 at $80–90B/year in federal-state investment (consistent with Section 5.5). This figure is partially offset by: increased lifetime tax revenue from higher educational attainment (Georgetown estimates $450,000 in additional federal tax revenue per bachelor's degree holder over a lifetime); reduced dependence on for-profit colleges (which cost federal loan programs $15-20B/year in defaults on degrees with poor labor market outcomes); and the macro return from a more educated workforce — Goldin and Katz (Harvard) estimate that each additional year of average educational attainment raises annual GDP by approximately 0.4%. Germany's free tuition reform (2014) increased enrollment 12% within three years with no measurable quality decline. The $1.77T existing student debt burden is being partially discharged through default and forgiveness programs regardless — front-loading free tuition stops the hemorrhage before it starts.

**10e. Lifelong learning infrastructure.**

Establish a federal Right to Learn account: every citizen receives $5,000 in lifetime learning credits (replenished on a 10-year cycle), usable for accredited education, vocational training, professional certification, or apprenticeships at any age. Credits are portable across states and institutions. The aim is to decouple education from the front-loaded 18-22 model and recognize that technological change and economic restructuring require continuous skill development.

*Precedent*: Singapore's SkillsFuture program provides every citizen over 25 with S$500 in annual credits for approved courses, plus additional subsidies for priority skills. Uptake has exceeded 600,000 Singaporeans annually. France's Compte Personnel de Formation (CPF) provides every worker with €500 annually (€800 for less-qualified workers) in a portable training account. Both systems are credited with improving labor force adaptability during technological transitions.

*Fiscal impact*: A $5,000/decade universal credit for all working-age adults, with observed uptake rates from comparable programs (10–20% annual utilization in Singapore and France), would produce annual expenditures of $13–26B. The McKinsey Global Institute estimates $550B/year in foregone productivity from skills mismatch in the U.S. workforce — workers in declining industries without the retraining pathways needed to transition to growing ones. U.S. Trade Adjustment Assistance evaluation data finds that workers who complete targeted retraining programs achieve 25–50% wage increases, with returns that exceed program costs within 3-5 years. The Right to Learn account is the infrastructure piece that makes lifelong skill adaptation a practical reality rather than an aspiration.

**10f. Universal pre-K and early childhood education.**

Establish federally funded, universal public pre-K for all 3- and 4-year-olds, available five full school days per week, staffed by teachers with bachelor's degrees in early childhood education compensated at K-12 salary parity. Operate through both public schools and community-based providers (Head Start, child care centers, nonprofit providers) with common quality standards and public oversight. Simultaneously expand subsidized childcare for children 0-2 through reformed Child Care and Development grants, with sliding-scale family contributions capped at 7% of income — the threshold at which childcare cost hardship is effectively eliminated in peer countries.

*Evidence*: The research base for high-quality early childhood education is among the strongest in all of social science. Nobel laureate economist James Heckman synthesizes multiple longitudinal studies to find consistent returns of $7–13 per dollar invested in high-quality early childhood programs, driven by improved educational attainment, reduced special education placement, lower incarceration rates, higher employment, and reduced healthcare costs across the life course. The Perry Preschool Project (1962–1967, longitudinal follow-up through age 40) found returns of $12.90 per dollar invested: participants were 40% more likely to hold steady employment, 40% less likely to have been arrested, and had significantly higher rates of high school graduation and homeownership. The Abecedarian Project (1972–1977) found 30-point IQ gains at age 5, higher rates of college attendance, and reduced teen parenthood among participants. Head Start evaluations show lasting benefits in school readiness, grade retention, special education placement, and high school graduation for participants from low-income families. The National Academies of Sciences 2018 consensus report — "Transforming the Workforce for Children Birth Through Age 8" — identifies the 0-8 window as the critical period for brain development and concludes that investment in this period has larger returns than equivalent investment at any later stage of education.

*International precedent*: France's école maternelle system, operational since 1882 and made compulsory from age 3 in 2019, achieves near-universal (99%) pre-K enrollment by operating through the existing public school infrastructure at approximately 0.5% of GDP — one of the lowest-cost universal pre-K systems in the world. Germany provides a legal entitlement to 35+ hours per week of subsidized Kita (integrated childcare and kindergarten) beginning at age 1, with 95%+ of 3-5 year olds enrolled. The Nordic countries (Sweden, Denmark, Norway, Finland) achieve 85–95% enrollment of 1-5 year olds in publicly subsidized early childhood programs at per-child costs 30–60% below U.S. market rates, due to public provision and professional staffing pipelines. The OECD average pre-K enrollment (ages 3-5) is 87%; the United States at 60% is below the OECD average and below every other G7 country. Canada's 2021 "$10/day childcare" national program — rolling out province by province through 2024 — has reduced childcare fees by 50%+ while increasing female labor force participation; Moody's Analytics projects a $1.50 GDP return per dollar invested in fee subsidies through increased workforce participation alone. The U.S. operates Head Start (serving approximately 833,000 children, age 3-5 from families in poverty, at $10.6B/year) as a targeted program — the international evidence is unanimous that universal programs achieve better outcomes than means-tested ones at comparable or lower per-child cost, because they attract higher-quality providers, avoid stigma, and build stronger political coalitions for sustained funding.

*Fiscal impact*: Universal pre-K for all approximately 8 million 3- and 4-year-olds at $8,000–12,000 per child annually (comparable to K-12 per-pupil spending in most states) would cost $64–96B gross, reduced to $25–35B net after consolidating existing programs (Head Start $10.6B, state pre-K programs $8-10B combined) and accounting for fiscal offsets: (a) reduced K-12 special education costs — early intervention reduces special education placement by 20–30%, saving $5,000–12,000 per child avoided placement; (b) reduced grade retention and dropout costs; and (c) increased female labor force participation — the "childcare cliff" that drives an estimated 300,000+ women from the workforce annually generates foregone wages and tax revenue estimated at $50–80B/year. Subsidized childcare for ages 0-2 would cost an additional $30–50B/year (net of existing CCDBG funding). Total Domain 10f investment: $55–85B/year. The Heckman return ($7–13 per dollar) suggests fiscal returns of $385–1,100B over the lifetime of each annual cohort served — the highest documented ROI of any social investment in the empirical literature.

---

## Domain 11: Healthcare

### The Problem

The United States spends more on healthcare than any other nation — $4.5 trillion in 2023, or 17.6% of GDP — while delivering worse outcomes than every peer country. Life expectancy is 77.5 years, 3-5 years below comparable nations. Infant mortality is the highest in the OECD. Approximately 27 million people have no insurance, and an estimated 40 million more are underinsured (insured but unable to afford the deductibles and copayments their plans require). Medical debt is the leading cause of personal bankruptcy, affecting an estimated 100 million Americans. The system fails by every metric that should matter: cost, access, outcomes, and equity.

**Administrative waste.** The U.S. healthcare system devotes approximately 34% of total expenditure to administration — billing, coding, claims processing, prior authorization, utilization review, and the overhead of maintaining thousands of distinct insurance products with different coverage rules. This is roughly double the administrative share of single-payer systems like Canada (17%) or Taiwan (less than 2% for their NHI program). A 2019 JAMA study estimated that simplifying administration to Canadian levels would save $628 billion annually — enough to cover every uninsured American with money remaining.

**Employer-based insurance as structural dysfunction.** The employer-based insurance system, an accident of WWII-era wage controls, ties healthcare access to employment status, suppresses labor mobility (job lock), creates massive administrative burden for businesses, disadvantages small businesses relative to large corporations, and produces coverage gaps during exactly the life events (job loss, illness, caregiving) when people most need healthcare. The system has no equivalent in any other developed country.

**Price opacity and market failure.** Healthcare is not a functioning market. Consumers cannot comparison shop during emergencies, do not choose their insurance (employers do), cannot evaluate quality, face opaque and widely variable pricing (the same procedure can cost 3-10x as much across hospitals in the same city), and are subject to surprise billing. The information asymmetry, urgency, and inelastic demand that characterize healthcare make consumer-market logic inapplicable. Every country that achieves better outcomes does so through some form of administered pricing or single-payer negotiation.

**Pharmaceutical pricing.** Americans pay 2-3 times as much as citizens of other developed countries for the same drugs. The pharmaceutical industry spends more on marketing ($30 billion annually) than on research ($25 billion in industry-funded R&D; most basic research is publicly funded through NIH). Medicare was prohibited from negotiating drug prices until 2022's IRA, and the negotiation authority remains limited to a small number of drugs with a decade-long timeline. Meanwhile, the U.S. patent system allows "evergreening" — incremental modifications that extend monopoly pricing well beyond the original patent term.

**Rural healthcare collapse.** Over 150 rural hospitals have closed since 2010. One in four remaining rural hospitals is at risk of closure. Over 80% of rural counties are designated health professional shortage areas. Rural Americans travel further, wait longer, and die younger than urban Americans. Maternal mortality in rural areas is roughly double the urban rate. The free-market model cannot sustain healthcare delivery in low-density areas because the economics don't support it — identical to how free markets cannot sustain rural mail delivery or broadband.

**The democratic consequence.** A population that is financially devastated by illness, that avoids care because of cost, that is tethered to unfulfilling jobs for insurance, and that experiences a 15-20 year life expectancy gap between the richest and poorest counties is not a population with the material security to sustain democratic engagement. Healthcare is not merely a policy domain — it is a precondition for the civic participation that every other domain in this proposal requires. Citizens in medical debt, facing bankruptcy from a cancer diagnosis, or rationing insulin do not have the bandwidth for citizens' assemblies.

### Proposed Reforms

**11a. Universal coverage through a public option with all-payer rate setting.**

Establish a public health insurance plan available to every American, funded through progressive premiums (income-based), with zero premiums for families under 200% of the federal poverty level. The public option competes with private insurance but has built-in advantages: no marketing costs, no profit extraction, and the ability to negotiate rates. Simultaneously, implement all-payer rate setting: all insurers (public and private) pay the same negotiated rate for each procedure, eliminating the cost-shifting and price opacity that define the current system.

*Why a public option rather than single-payer*: Single-payer is the more efficient system (lower administrative costs, simpler implementation, greater negotiating leverage). However, the political pathway to single-payer in the United States faces insuperable obstacles in the near term: the insurance industry employs approximately 2.7 million people, Medicare for All polled below 50% in most 2024 surveys when respondents were told it would eliminate private insurance, and no proposed single-payer bill has ever gotten a floor vote. A public option with all-payer rate setting achieves 80% of single-payer's efficiency gains while preserving consumer choice and creating a glide path: if the public option is genuinely better, enrollment will shift naturally over 10-20 years without forced displacement. This is how Taiwan, South Korea, and Germany evolved toward universal coverage.

*Precedent*: Germany's system is the closest model. Statutory health insurance covers 90% of the population through nonprofit "sickness funds," with private insurance available for higher earners. All payers negotiate standardized rates with provider associations. Administrative costs are approximately 5% of expenditure. Per-capita healthcare spending is roughly half of the U.S. level, with better outcomes on virtually every metric. Taiwan's single-payer NHI (established 1995) demonstrates that even a middle-income country can achieve universal coverage with under 2% administrative overhead when the political will exists.

*Fiscal impact*: A U.S. public option modeled on Germany's system would require $150–200B/year in premium subsidies for zero-premium low-income enrollees, offset by administrative savings from all-payer rate setting ($100–200B/year per RAND/Commonwealth Fund estimates). The 2019 JAMA study estimated $628B/year in savings from administrative simplification to Canadian levels — the public option + all-payer framework captures roughly one-third of this through standardized contracting and reduced insurer overhead. Net fiscal position: approximately cost-neutral in year 1, with $50–100B/year in net public savings by year 5 as enrollment grows and administrative efficiency improves.

**11b. Negotiated drug pricing and patent reform.**

Extend Medicare drug price negotiation to all drugs (not just 10, as under the IRA), and apply negotiated prices to all payers (public and private). Cap annual out-of-pocket prescription costs at $2,000. Reform patent law to prohibit evergreening (minor modifications that extend monopoly pricing without meaningful therapeutic improvement). Require that drugs developed with significant public funding (NIH-funded basic research) include a reasonable pricing clause in the license.

*Precedent*: Every other developed country negotiates drug prices. The UK's NICE evaluates cost-effectiveness and sets maximum reimbursement. Canada's PMPRB caps prices at the median of comparator countries. Australia's PBS requires demonstrated cost-effectiveness for public subsidy. American prices average 2.56 times those of 32 OECD countries for the same molecules. The negotiation mechanisms are well-understood; what is missing is political will against an industry that spends more on lobbying ($374 million in 2022) than any other sector.

*Evidence*: The CBO estimated that the IRA's limited negotiation provisions would save $100 billion over 10 years for 10 drugs. Extrapolating to all drugs and all payers, comprehensive negotiation could save $300-500 billion annually.

*Fiscal impact*: The IRA's limited drug negotiation saves $10B/year (CBO: $100B/10 years for 10 drugs). Full extension to all drugs and all payers: $300–500B/year in savings, based on the 2.56x average U.S. price premium over 32 OECD comparator countries. Annual out-of-pocket cap at $2,000 costs $20–30B/year in additional federal subsidies. Evergreening prohibition: estimated $8–15B/year in savings as brand-name monopolies shorten. Total net impact of full drug pricing reform: $270–480B/year in savings across public and private payers — making this the single highest-value reform in the healthcare domain.

**11c. Primary care and rural health infrastructure.**

Establish a National Health Service Corps expansion that fully funds medical education (tuition, living expenses) for physicians, nurse practitioners, and physician assistants who commit to 10 years of practice in underserved areas (rural counties, low-income urban areas, tribal lands). Fund community health centers as the primary care backbone for underserved populations, targeting one federally qualified health center per 15,000 people in designated shortage areas. Establish telehealth as a permanent, fully reimbursed modality (not a pandemic exception) to extend specialist access to rural areas.

*Precedent*: Cuba's primary care system — despite severe resource constraints — achieves a life expectancy of 79 years (higher than the U.S.) through universal access to community-based primary care physicians, with one doctor per 150 families. The model demonstrates that primary care investment, not high-tech hospital spending, drives population health outcomes. Costa Rica's EBAIS system (community health teams) achieves life expectancy of 80 years at a fraction of U.S. per-capita spending. Within the U.S., the existing NHSC and FQHC programs are well-regarded but chronically underfunded — expansion, not invention, is required.

*Fiscal impact*: Full NHSC expansion — loan repayment and stipends for physicians and nurse practitioners committing to shortage-area practice: $8–12B/year. Fully funded medical education for NHSC-committed clinicians (forgivable tuition + living expenses): $5–10B/year. FQHC network expansion to one federally qualified health center per 15,000 shortage-area residents (current: approximately 1 per 30,000): $10–18B/year in additional operating grants and construction funding. Permanent telehealth reimbursement parity and infrastructure: $3–5B/year. Total: $26–45B/year. Against this investment, rural healthcare deserts currently generate an estimated $6,000 in excess annual healthcare spending per rural resident through delayed care, emergency substitution, and higher mortality — affecting 60+ million rural Americans. The preventive return alone exceeds the investment.

**11d. Administrative simplification.**

Mandate standardized billing codes, universal claims forms, and automated prior authorization across all payers. Establish a national patient identifier (linked to the digital identity infrastructure in Domain 4) to eliminate the fragmented medical record system that causes an estimated 250,000 deaths annually from medical errors (per Johns Hopkins, many attributable to information gaps). Require real-time price transparency: patients must be able to see the actual price they will pay for any non-emergency procedure before agreeing to it, with prices published in machine-readable format.

*Evidence*: The Affordable Care Act's standardized electronic transactions saved an estimated $3-5 billion annually. Extending standardization to prior authorization alone would save an estimated $31 billion annually (CAQH index). Taiwan's single IT system processes claims in under 24 hours with a staff of 700 people — serving 23 million patients.

*Fiscal impact*: Universal prior authorization automation: $31B/year in savings (CAQH index, confirmed by CMS implementation data). Universal electronic billing standards: $13–25B/year in savings (CMS estimates; the ACA's partial standards saved $3–5B/year — full implementation recovers 3–5x). National patient identifier enabling coordinated care: $10–20B/year in reduced duplicate testing, redundant imaging, and medical error costs (Johns Hopkins estimates 250,000 annual preventable deaths from information gaps; reducing this 50% at average hospitalization cost avoids $10–20B/year). Real-time price transparency implementation: $500M–1B/year in federal administration. Total administrative simplification savings: $55–77B/year — recoverable without reducing a single clinical service, and achievable through regulatory mandates rather than new program spending.

**11e. Mental health parity and integration.**

Enforce genuine mental health parity: mental health and substance use treatment must be covered at the same level as physical health, with the same network adequacy requirements, the same prior authorization standards, and the same out-of-pocket cost structure. Integrate behavioral health into primary care through the Collaborative Care Model (psychiatric consultant embedded in primary care practice), which has been shown in 90+ randomized controlled trials to improve depression outcomes by 50% compared to usual care while reducing total healthcare costs.

*Precedent*: The Mental Health Parity and Addiction Equity Act (2008) required parity in theory; enforcement has been minimal. A 2023 HHS report found that insurers still deny mental health claims at 2-3 times the rate of physical health claims. The enforcement mechanism, not the principle, is what needs reform. The UK's Improving Access to Psychological Therapies (IAPT) program, which provides evidence-based therapy within 6 weeks to anyone who self-refers, treats over 1 million people annually and has measurably reduced depression and anxiety prevalence.

*Fiscal impact*: Genuine parity enforcement — federal agency ramp-up and state enforcement grants: $500M–1B/year. Collaborative Care Model integration into primary care practices nationally: $10–15B/year in adjusted federal reimbursement. U.S.-scale equivalent of IAPT (evidence-based therapy accessible within 6 weeks for all who need it): $20–35B/year. Net of reduced emergency department utilization, reduced hospitalization, and productivity restoration, the investment is more than offset: NAMI estimates $193B/year in lost earnings from untreated mental illness; NIH data shows $500B/year in excess healthcare costs associated with undertreated behavioral health conditions. A 3–5x return on evidence-based mental health investment has been documented across multiple health economics studies.

**11f. Dental, vision, and long-term care.**

The three largest gaps in the U.S. healthcare system — areas where the market has systematically failed and where the public option as described in 11a leaves significant unmet need — are dental care, vision care, and long-term care for older and disabled Americans.

*Dental exclusion*. Traditional Medicare has never covered routine dental care. The Inflation Reduction Act added limited dental coverage for specific conditions, but routine dental care — cleanings, fillings, extractions, dentures — remains uncovered for 66 million Medicare beneficiaries. Approximately 74 million Americans have no dental insurance. The CDC estimates that 34% of adults aged 65+ have lost all their teeth, a rate that is dramatically higher among low-income and rural populations. Untreated dental disease is not cosmetic — periodontal disease is associated with heart disease, diabetes complications, and preterm birth; dental infections kill. The U.S. is the only OECD country that treats dental care as categorically separate from healthcare for public coverage purposes.

*Vision exclusion*. Medicare does not cover routine vision care or eyeglasses. Approximately 22 million Americans 40+ have some form of vision impairment; cataracts and macular degeneration are the leading causes of preventable blindness. Untreated vision impairment contributes to 2.3 million preventable falls annually and reduces quality of life, employment participation, and driving safety. Low-income Americans often delay vision correction for years due to cost.

*Long-term care catastrophe*. The United States has no functional long-term care system. Medicare covers only 100 days of skilled nursing care post-hospitalization. Medicaid covers long-term care — but only after near-total asset spend-down, effectively wiping out the life savings of middle-class families before they qualify. Private long-term care insurance covers approximately 7.5 million people and is in market failure (premiums have risen 25–35% in recent years as insurers exit). The average annual cost of a nursing home private room: $108,405. Assisted living: $60,000/year. Home health aide (full-time): $61,776/year. A devastating and predictable expense for which the vast majority of Americans have no preparation — and no public backstop until impoverishment.

**Proposed reforms for 11f**: Incorporate routine dental and vision coverage into Medicare and the public option (as proposed in Build Back Better and multiple Medicare expansion bills). Fund through modest premium adjustment and from drug price negotiation savings (Domain 11b). Establish a federal long-term care public benefit — not means-tested to impoverishment — modeled on Germany's long-term care insurance (Pflegeversicherung), Japan's universal long-term care insurance (kaigo hoken), or the Netherlands' long-term care act (WLZ). Fund through a dedicated payroll contribution (Germany funds its system at 3.4% of wages, shared employer-employee) or from Domain 5 revenue. Prioritize home and community-based care over institutional care — empirically preferred by patients and significantly cheaper.

*Precedent*: Germany's Pflegeversicherung (established 1995) provides universal long-term care insurance — regardless of income — through a 3.4% payroll contribution. Benefits range from €316/month (care level 1) to €2,095/month (care level 5), with higher benefits for home care to incentivize family and community caregiving. Japan's universal long-term care insurance (kaigo hoken, 2000) covers everyone over 65, financed 50% by premiums and 50% by general taxation, with a 10% co-payment. Enrollment eliminated the institutionalization of elderly people who did not need full nursing care — a pattern that had produced overcrowded hospitals serving a custodial rather than clinical function. The UK's NHS provides dental care through NHS dentists at a flat subsidized rate (Band 1–3 treatments: £26.80–£306.80 in 2024) — not free, but dramatically more accessible than the U.S. system. Canada's 2023 Canadian Dental Care Plan provides coverage for uninsured Canadians with household income below $90,000, using an existing income-based public coverage mechanism. Australia's Medicare includes optometry services and subsidized dental for children and concession card holders.

*Fiscal impact*: Adding dental and vision to Medicare and the public option: $35–55B/year (CMS estimates for full Medicare dental benefit; prior CBO score of Build Back Better dental provision was $238B over 10 years = $24B/year for Medicare only; broader public option inclusion adds $10–30B/year). Long-term care public benefit modeled on Germany's system at comparable per-capita spending ($1,200/year per covered individual): $200–250B/year for 170+ million working-age contributors and 66 million Medicare beneficiaries — offset by elimination of Medicaid long-term care spend-down ($100B+/year) and private long-term care insurance market distortions. Net new federal long-term care cost: $50–100B/year above current Medicaid LTC spending, funded by a dedicated 2–3% payroll contribution. This is the most politically underaddressed healthcare gap in American life — the financial catastrophe awaiting the majority of the middle class as they age.

---

## Domain 12: Infrastructure

### The Problem

The American Society of Civil Engineers grades U.S. infrastructure at C- overall, estimating a $2.59 trillion investment gap over the next decade. The 2021 Infrastructure Investment and Jobs Act addressed roughly one-third of this gap; two-thirds remains unfunded. The consequences are not abstract — they are measured in collapsed bridges, contaminated water, power grid failures, and the economic productivity lost to congestion, unreliable transit, and digital exclusion. Infrastructure is not a technical domain separate from democratic renewal; it is the physical substrate on which civic and economic life depends.

**Broadband as democratic infrastructure.** Approximately 24 million Americans lack access to broadband internet (FCC's revised 2024 estimate; independent researchers suggest the actual figure may be double). The divide is geographic — rural areas, tribal lands, and low-income urban neighborhoods — and it is consequential: remote work, telehealth, online education, digital government services, and civic participation increasingly require reliable internet. During the pandemic, an estimated 15-16 million students could not fully participate in remote schooling. A democracy that moves government services online while leaving tens of millions without access has created a new class of disenfranchisement.

**Energy grid vulnerability.** The U.S. electrical grid is a patchwork of aging infrastructure — average transmission line age exceeds 40 years — governed by a fragmented regulatory structure (3,000+ utilities, 50 state commissions, FERC, NERC). The February 2021 Texas grid collapse killed an estimated 246 people and caused $195 billion in damage. California's wildfire-driven shutoffs affect millions annually. The grid was designed for centralized fossil fuel generation and cannot efficiently integrate distributed renewables, battery storage, or the load increases from electrification of vehicles and heating without fundamental modernization. Grid reliability is not a climate issue alone — it is a national security and public safety issue.

**Water infrastructure decay.** An estimated 6-10 million American homes still receive water through lead service lines. The EPA estimates that water infrastructure needs $625 billion in investment over the next 20 years. Flint, Michigan (2014-2019), Jackson, Mississippi (2022), and dozens of smaller crises have demonstrated that contaminated or unreliable water disproportionately affects low-income communities and communities of color. The Lead and Copper Rule Improvements (2024) mandate lead pipe replacement but on a 10-year timeline with uncertain local funding.

**Public transit underfunding.** The U.S. spends roughly $50 per capita on public transit annually, compared to $360 in France and $430 in Switzerland. Outside a handful of legacy systems (New York, Chicago, Washington, San Francisco), most American cities have minimal or nonexistent public transit. This is a policy choice, not a geographic inevitability — cities of comparable density in Europe and Asia have comprehensive transit networks. The consequence is car dependency, which functions as a regressive tax: transportation is the second-largest household expense in the U.S. (average $12,295/year), and families without cars in transit-poor areas face severe constraints on employment, healthcare access, and civic participation.

**The democratic consequence.** Citizens who cannot access reliable internet cannot participate in digital democracy. Communities whose water is poisoned or whose power grid fails during extreme weather face acute crises that crowd out civic engagement. Workers who spend 2-3 hours daily commuting on inadequate transit or congested highways have less time and energy for civic participation. Infrastructure neglect is not neutral — it falls hardest on communities already marginalized, compounding the inequality that undermines democratic legitimacy.

### Proposed Reforms

**12a. Universal broadband as a regulated utility.**

Classify broadband internet as essential infrastructure and regulate it accordingly. Establish a federal broadband standard (100/100 Mbps symmetric) and fund last-mile deployment to all unserved and underserved areas through a combination of federal grants, municipal broadband authorization, and open-access fiber mandates (where public funding builds the fiber, multiple ISPs can offer service over it). Prohibit state laws that prevent municipalities from building their own broadband networks (currently 18 states restrict municipal broadband).

*Precedent*: South Korea achieved 99.9% broadband penetration through government-directed investment and open-access regulation, with average speeds 3-4 times faster than the U.S. at lower consumer cost. Finland declared broadband a legal right in 2010. Within the U.S., Chattanooga's municipal fiber network (EPB) delivers gigabit service at $67.99/month, outperforms every private ISP in the region, generates a net surplus for the city, and has been credited with $2.7 billion in local economic benefit over a decade. The model works; what prevents replication is incumbent ISP lobbying and state-level preemption laws.

*Fiscal impact*: The FCC's Broadband Equity, Access, and Deployment (BEAD) program allocates $42.5B for last-mile deployment — roughly half the estimated $65–100B needed for full universal coverage. Ongoing low-income access subsidies (Affordable Connectivity Program–equivalent): $5–8B/year. Economic return: universal broadband generates an estimated $9.1B/year in productivity from reduced commuting and remote work expansion (Brookings), $6.3B/year in additional federal and state tax revenue, and $5–10B/year in healthcare savings through telehealth substitution. The 15–16 million students without reliable connectivity during pandemic remote learning represent a generational educational loss whose economic cost exceeds the capital investment. 10-year economic return on broadband universalization: $150–200B against a $65–100B capital outlay.

**12b. Energy grid modernization and resilience.**

Fund a 10-year, $400 billion federal grid modernization program focused on three objectives: (1) transmission capacity expansion to connect renewable generation zones to population centers, (2) grid hardening against extreme weather (undergrounding vulnerable lines, upgrading substations, deploying microgrids for critical facilities), and (3) distributed energy resource integration (standardized interconnection rules for rooftop solar, battery storage, and vehicle-to-grid). Establish regional grid reliability standards with enforceable consequences for failure. Consolidate ERCOT (Texas) into the national interconnection to prevent isolated grid collapse.
